	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Ludwig van Beethoven</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"Beethoven" redirects here. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Beethoven_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Beethoven (disambiguation)">Beethoven (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">18th and 19th-century German classical and romantic composer</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">

</p>

<p><b>Ludwig van Beethoven</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;l&#39; in &#39;lie&#39;">l</span><span title="/ʊ/: &#39;u&#39; in &#39;push&#39;">ʊ</span><span title="&#39;d&#39; in &#39;dye&#39;">d</span><span title="&#39;v&#39; in &#39;vie&#39;">v</span><span title="/ɪ/: &#39;i&#39; in &#39;kit&#39;">ɪ</span><span title="/ɡ/: &#39;g&#39; in &#39;guy&#39;">ɡ</span></span><span class="wrap"> </span><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="&#39;v&#39; in &#39;vie&#39;">v</span><span title="/æ/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;bad&#39;">æ</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span></span><span class="wrap"> </span><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;b&#39; in &#39;buy&#39;">b</span><span title="/eɪ/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;face&#39;">eɪ</span><span title="&#39;t&#39; in &#39;tie&#39;">t</span></span>(<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="&#39;h&#39; in &#39;hi&#39;">h</span></span>)<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/oʊ/: &#39;o&#39; in &#39;code&#39;">oʊ</span><span title="&#39;v&#39; in &#39;vie&#39;">v</span><span title="/ən/: &#39;on&#39; in &#39;button&#39;">ən</span></span>/</a></span>&#32;<span class="nowrap" style="font-size:85%">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:En-LudwigVanBeethoven.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" decoding="async" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20" /></a></span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c7/En-LudwigVanBeethoven.ogg" class="internal" title="En-LudwigVanBeethoven.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</span></span>; <small>German:&#32;</small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Standard_German" title="Help:IPA/Standard German">[ˈluːtvɪç fan ˈbeːthoːfn̩]</a></span>&#32;<span class="nowrap" style="font-size:85%">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:De-Ludwig_van_Beethoven.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" decoding="async" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20" /></a></span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/ae/De-Ludwig_van_Beethoven.ogg" class="internal" title="De-Ludwig van Beethoven.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</span>; baptised 17&#160;December 1770<sup id="cite_ref-birth_date?_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-birth_date?-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>&#160;&#8211;&#32;26&#160;March 1827) was a German <a href="/wiki/Composer" title="Composer">composer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pianist" title="Pianist">pianist</a>. A crucial figure in the transition between the <a href="/wiki/Classical_period_(music)" title="Classical period (music)">classical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Romantic_music" title="Romantic music">romantic</a> eras in <a href="/wiki/Classical_music" title="Classical music">classical music</a>, he remains one of the most recognized and influential musicians of this period, and is considered to be one of the greatest composers of all time.
</p><p>Beethoven was born in <a href="/wiki/Bonn" title="Bonn">Bonn</a>, the capital of the <a href="/wiki/Electorate_of_Cologne" title="Electorate of Cologne">Electorate of Cologne</a>, and part of the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a>. He displayed his musical talents at an early age and was vigorously taught by his father <a href="/wiki/Johann_van_Beethoven" title="Johann van Beethoven">Johann van Beethoven</a>, and was later taught by composer and conductor <a href="/wiki/Christian_Gottlob_Neefe" title="Christian Gottlob Neefe">Christian Gottlob Neefe</a>. At age 21, he moved to <a href="/wiki/Vienna" title="Vienna">Vienna</a> and studied composition with <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Haydn" title="Joseph Haydn">Joseph Haydn</a>. Beethoven then gained a reputation as a virtuoso pianist, and was soon courted by <a href="/wiki/Karl_Alois,_Prince_Lichnowsky" title="Karl Alois, Prince Lichnowsky">Prince Lichnowsky</a> for compositions, which resulted in <a href="/wiki/Piano_Trios,_Op._1_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Trios, Op. 1 (Beethoven)">Opus 1</a> in 1795.
</p><p>The piece was a great critical and commercial success, and was followed by <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._1_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 1 (Beethoven)">Symphony No. 1</a> in 1800. This composition was distinguished for its frequent use of <i><a href="/wiki/Dynamics_(music)#Changes" title="Dynamics (music)">sforzandi</a></i>, as well as sudden shifts in tonal centers that were uncommon for traditional symphonic form, and the prominent, more independent use of wind instruments.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> In 1801, he also gained notoriety for his six <a href="/wiki/String_Quartets_Nos._1%E2%80%936,_Op._18_(Beethoven)" title="String Quartets Nos. 1–6, Op. 18 (Beethoven)">String Quartets</a> and for the ballet <i><a href="/wiki/The_Creatures_of_Prometheus" title="The Creatures of Prometheus">The Creatures of Prometheus</a></i>. During this period, his hearing began to deteriorate, but he continued to conduct, premiering his <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._3_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 3 (Beethoven)">third</a> and <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._5_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 5 (Beethoven)">fifth</a> <a href="/wiki/Symphony" title="Symphony">symphonies</a> in 1804 and 1808, respectively. His condition worsened to almost complete deafness by 1811, and he then gave up performing and appearing in public.
</p><p>During this period of self exile, Beethoven composed many of his most admired works; his <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._7_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 7 (Beethoven)">seventh</a> symphony premiered in 1813, with its second movement, <i>Allegretto</i>, achieving widespread critical acclaim.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> He composed the piece <i><a href="/wiki/Missa_solemnis_(Beethoven)" title="Missa solemnis (Beethoven)">Missa Solemnis</a></i> for a number of years until it premiered 1824, which preceded his <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._9_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 9 (Beethoven)">ninth</a> symphony, with the latter gaining fame for being among the first examples of a <a href="/wiki/Choral_symphony" title="Choral symphony">choral symphony</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-bonds24837_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bonds24837-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> In 1826, his <a href="/wiki/String_Quartet_No._14_(Beethoven)" title="String Quartet No. 14 (Beethoven)">fourteenth String Quartet</a> was noted for having seven linked movements played without a break, and is considered the final major piece performed before <a href="/wiki/Death_of_Ludwig_van_Beethoven" title="Death of Ludwig van Beethoven">his death</a> a year later.
</p><p>His career is conventionally divided into early, middle, and late periods; the "early" period is typically seen to last until 1802, the "middle" period from 1802 to 1812, and the "late" period from 1812 to his death in 1827. During his life, he composed nine <a href="/wiki/Symphony" title="Symphony">symphonies</a>; five <a href="/wiki/Piano_concerto" title="Piano concerto">piano concertos</a>; one <a href="/wiki/Violin_concerto" title="Violin concerto">violin concerto</a>; thirty-two <a href="/wiki/Piano_sonata" title="Piano sonata">piano sonatas</a>; sixteen <a href="/wiki/String_quartet" title="String quartet">string quartets</a>; two <a href="/wiki/Mass_(music)" title="Mass (music)">masses</a>; and the <a href="/wiki/Opera" title="Opera">opera</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Fidelio" title="Fidelio">Fidelio</a></i>. Other works, like <a href="/wiki/F%C3%BCr_Elise" title="Für Elise">Für Elise</a>, were discovered after his death, and are also considered historical musical achievements. Beethoven's legacy is characterized for his innovative compositions, namely through the combinations of vocals and instruments, and also for widening the scope of sonata, symphony, concerto, and quartet,<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> while he is also noted for his <a href="/wiki/Beethoven_and_his_contemporaries" title="Beethoven and his contemporaries">troublesome relationship with his contemporaries</a>.
</p>

</ul>
</li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-13"><a href="#Music"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">Music</span></a>
<ul>

</ul>
</li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-16"><a href="#Character"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Character</span></a></li>

</div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Life_and_career">Life and career</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Background_and_early_life">Background and early life</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Beethoven_house_of_birth_Bonn_2008.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Beethoven_house_of_birth_Bonn_2008.jpg/170px-Beethoven_house_of_birth_Bonn_2008.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="281" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Beethoven_house_of_birth_Bonn_2008.jpg/255px-Beethoven_house_of_birth_Bonn_2008.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Beethoven_house_of_birth_Bonn_2008.jpg/340px-Beethoven_house_of_birth_Bonn_2008.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1996" data-file-height="3302" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Beethoven_house_of_birth_Bonn_2008.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Beethoven's birthplace at <i lang="de" title="German language text">Bonngasse&#160;20</i>, now the <a href="/wiki/Beethoven_House" title="Beethoven House">Beethoven House</a> museum</div></div></div>
<p>Beethoven was the grandson of <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_van_Beethoven_(1712%E2%80%931773)" title="Ludwig van Beethoven (1712–1773)">Ludwig van Beethoven</a> (1712–1773), a musician from the town of <a href="/wiki/Mechelen" title="Mechelen">Mechelen</a> in the Austrian <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Brabant" title="Duchy of Brabant">Duchy of Brabant</a> (in what is now the <a href="/wiki/Flemish_region" class="mw-redirect" title="Flemish region">Flemish region</a> of Belgium) who had moved to <a href="/wiki/Bonn" title="Bonn">Bonn</a> at the age of 21.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroveOnlinesection_1_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroveOnlinesection_1-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper2008407_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper2008407-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Ludwig was employed as a <a href="/wiki/Bass_(voice_type)" title="Bass (voice type)">bass</a> singer at the court of <a href="/wiki/Clemens_August_of_Bavaria" title="Clemens August of Bavaria">Clemens August</a>, <a href="/wiki/Electorate_of_Cologne" title="Electorate of Cologne">Archbishop-Elector of Cologne</a>, eventually rising to become, in 1761, <i lang="de" title="German language text"><a href="/wiki/Kapellmeister" title="Kapellmeister">Kapellmeister</a></i> (music director) and thereafter the pre-eminent musician in Bonn. The portrait he commissioned of himself towards the end of his life remained displayed in his grandson's rooms as a talisman of his musical heritage.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESwafford201412–17_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESwafford201412–17-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Ludwig had one son, <a href="/wiki/Johann_van_Beethoven" title="Johann van Beethoven">Johann</a> (1740–1792), who worked as a tenor in the same musical establishment and gave keyboard and <a href="/wiki/Violin" title="Violin">violin</a> lessons to supplement his income.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroveOnlinesection_1_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroveOnlinesection_1-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Johann married <a href="/wiki/Maria_Magdalena_Keverich" title="Maria Magdalena Keverich">Maria Magdalena Keverich</a> in 1767; she was the daughter of Johann Heinrich Keverich (1701–1751), who had been the head chef at the court of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Diocese_of_Trier" title="Roman Catholic Diocese of Trier">Archbishopric of Trier</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer192149_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer192149-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Beethoven was born of this marriage in Bonn. There is no authentic record of the date of his birth; however, the registry of his baptism, in a <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic</a> service at the Parish of St. Regius on 17&#160;December 1770, survives.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> As children of that era were traditionally baptised the day after birth in the Catholic Rhine country, and it is known that Beethoven's family and his teacher <a href="/wiki/Johann_Georg_Albrechtsberger" title="Johann Georg Albrechtsberger">Johann Albrechtsberger</a> celebrated his birthday on 16&#160;December, most scholars accept 16&#160;December 1770 as his date of birth.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer192153_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer192153-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolomon2000chapter_1_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolomon2000chapter_1-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Of the seven children born to Johann van Beethoven, only Ludwig, the second-born, and two younger brothers survived infancy. <a href="/wiki/Kaspar_Anton_Karl_van_Beethoven" title="Kaspar Anton Karl van Beethoven">Kaspar Anton Karl</a> was born on 8&#160;April 1774, and <a href="/wiki/Nikolaus_Johann_van_Beethoven" title="Nikolaus Johann van Beethoven">Nikolaus Johann</a>, the youngest, was born on 2&#160;October 1776.<sup id="cite_ref-Stanley7_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stanley7-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Beethoven's first music teacher was his father. He later had other local teachers: the court <a href="/wiki/Organist" title="Organist">organist</a> Gilles van den Eeden (d. 1782), Tobias Friedrich Pfeiffer (a family friend, who provided keyboard tuition), and Franz Rovantini (a relative, who instructed him in playing the violin and <a href="/wiki/Viola" title="Viola">viola</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroveOnlinesection_1_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroveOnlinesection_1-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> From the outset his tuition regime, which began in his fifth year, was harsh and intensive, often reducing him to tears; with the involvement of the insomniac Pfeiffer there were irregular late-night sessions with the young Beethoven being dragged from his bed to the keyboard.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESwafford201422,_32_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESwafford201422,_32-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> His musical talent was obvious at a young age. Johann, aware of <a href="/wiki/Leopold_Mozart" title="Leopold Mozart">Leopold Mozart</a>'s successes in this area (with his son <a href="/wiki/Wolfgang_Amadeus_Mozart" title="Wolfgang Amadeus Mozart">Wolfgang</a> and daughter <a href="/wiki/Maria_Anna_Mozart" title="Maria Anna Mozart">Nannerl</a>), attempted to promote his son as a <a href="/wiki/Child_prodigy" title="Child prodigy">child prodigy</a>, claiming that Beethoven was six (he was seven) on the posters for his first public performance in March 1778.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer192159_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer192159-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_published_compositions">First published compositions</span></h3>
<p>Some time after 1779, Beethoven began his studies with his most important teacher in Bonn, <a href="/wiki/Christian_Gottlob_Neefe" title="Christian Gottlob Neefe">Christian Gottlob Neefe</a>, who was appointed the Court's Organist in that year.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer192167_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer192167-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Neefe taught him composition, and by March 1783 had helped him write his first published composition: a set of keyboard variations (<a href="/wiki/WoO" title="WoO">WoO</a> 63).<sup id="cite_ref-Stanley7_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stanley7-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Beethoven soon began working with Neefe as assistant organist, at first unpaid (1781), and then as a paid employee (1784) of the court chapel conducted by the Kapellmeister <a href="/wiki/Andrea_Luchesi" title="Andrea Luchesi">Andrea Luchesi</a>. His <a href="/wiki/Three_Piano_Sonatas,_WoO_47_(Beethoven)" title="Three Piano Sonatas, WoO 47 (Beethoven)">first three piano sonatas</a>, named "<i lang="de" title="German language text"><a href="/wiki/Kurf%C3%BCrst" class="mw-redirect" title="Kurfürst">Kurfürst</a></i>" ("Elector") for their dedication to the Elector <a href="/wiki/Maximilian_Friedrich_von_K%C3%B6nigsegg-Rothenfels" title="Maximilian Friedrich von Königsegg-Rothenfels">Maximilian Friedrich</a> (1708–1784), were published in 1783. Maximilian Frederick noticed his talent early, and subsidised and encouraged the young man's musical studies.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer192171–74_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer192171–74-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Thirteen-year-old_Beethoven.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Thirteen-year-old_Beethoven.jpg/170px-Thirteen-year-old_Beethoven.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="211" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Thirteen-year-old_Beethoven.jpg/255px-Thirteen-year-old_Beethoven.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Thirteen-year-old_Beethoven.jpg/340px-Thirteen-year-old_Beethoven.jpg 2x" data-file-width="912" data-file-height="1132" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Thirteen-year-old_Beethoven.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A portrait of the 13-year-old Beethoven by an unknown Bonn master (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;1783</span>)</div></div></div>
<p>Maximilian Frederick's successor as the Elector of Bonn was <a href="/wiki/Archduke_Maximilian_Francis_of_Austria" title="Archduke Maximilian Francis of Austria">Maximilian Francis</a>, the youngest son of Empress <a href="/wiki/Maria_Theresa" title="Maria Theresa">Maria Theresa</a> of Austria, and he brought notable changes to Bonn. Echoing changes made in Vienna by his brother <a href="/wiki/Joseph_II_of_Austria" class="mw-redirect" title="Joseph II of Austria">Joseph</a>, he introduced reforms based on <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Enlightenment philosophy</a>, with increased support for education and the arts. The teenage Beethoven was almost certainly influenced by these changes. He may also have been influenced at this time by ideas prominent in <a href="/wiki/Freemasonry" title="Freemasonry">freemasonry</a>, as Neefe and others around Beethoven were members of the local chapter of the <a href="/wiki/Illuminati" title="Illuminati">Order of the Illuminati</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200815_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200815-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In December 1786, Beethoven travelled to Vienna, at his employer's expense, for the first time, apparently in the hope of studying with Mozart.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> The details of <a href="/wiki/Beethoven_and_Mozart" title="Beethoven and Mozart">their relationship</a> are uncertain, including whether they actually met.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200823_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200823-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Having learned that his mother was ill, Beethoven returned quickly to Bonn in May 1787. His mother died shortly thereafter, and his father lapsed deeper into <a href="/wiki/Alcoholism" title="Alcoholism">alcoholism</a>. As a result, he became responsible for the care of his two younger brothers, and spent the next five years in Bonn.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200824_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200824-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>He was introduced in these years to several people who became important in his life. Franz Wegeler, a young medical student, introduced him to the von Breuning family (one of whose daughters Wegeler eventually married). He often visited the von Breuning household, where he taught piano to some of the children. Here he encountered German and classical literature. The von Breuning family environment was less stressful than his own, which was increasingly dominated by his father's decline.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200816_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200816-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> He also came to the attention of <a href="/wiki/Count_Ferdinand_Ernst_Gabriel_von_Waldstein" title="Count Ferdinand Ernst Gabriel von Waldstein">Count Ferdinand von Waldstein</a>, who became a lifelong friend and financial supporter.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer1921102_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer1921102-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1789 Beethoven obtained a legal order by which half of his father's salary was paid directly to him for support of the family.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer1921104_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer1921104-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> He also contributed further to the family's income by playing <a href="/wiki/Viola" title="Viola">viola</a> in the court orchestra. This familiarised him with a variety of operas, including three by Mozart that were performed at court in this period. He also befriended <a href="/wiki/Anton_Reicha" title="Anton Reicha">Anton Reicha</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Flute" title="Flute">flautist</a> and violinist of about his own age who was a nephew of the court orchestra's conductor, <a href="/wiki/Josef_Reicha" title="Josef Reicha">Josef Reicha</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer1921105–09_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer1921105–09-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>From 1790 to 1792, he composed a significant number of works (none were published at the time, and most are now listed as "WoO", <a href="/wiki/WoO" title="WoO">works without opus number</a>) that demonstrated his growing range and maturity. <a href="/wiki/Musicologist" class="mw-redirect" title="Musicologist">Musicologists</a> have identified a theme similar to those of his <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._3_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 3 (Beethoven)">Third Symphony</a> in a set of variations written in 1791.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200835–41_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200835–41-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> It was probably on Neefe's recommendation that Beethoven received his first commissions; the municipal leaders in Bonn had commissioned cantatas to mark the occasion of the death in 1790 of <a href="/wiki/Joseph_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Joseph II, Holy Roman Emperor">Joseph II</a> and the subsequent accession of <a href="/wiki/Leopold_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Leopold II, Holy Roman Emperor">Leopold II</a> as <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Holy Roman Emperor">Holy Roman Emperor</a>. The two <i>Emperor Cantatas</i> (WoO 87, WoO 88) he scored were never performed at the time and they remained lost until the 1880s. But they were, according to <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Brahms" title="Johannes Brahms">Brahms</a>, distinctively "Beethoven through and through" and as such prophetic of the tragic style which would mark his music as distinct from the classical tradition.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>He was probably first introduced to <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Haydn" title="Joseph Haydn">Joseph Haydn</a> in late 1790, when the latter was travelling to London and stopped in Bonn around Christmas time.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200835_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200835-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> A year and a half later, they met in Bonn on Haydn's return trip from London to Vienna in July 1792, when Beethoven played in the orchestra at the <a href="/wiki/Redoute,_Bad_Godesberg" title="Redoute, Bad Godesberg">Redoute in Godesberg</a>. It is likely that arrangements were made at that time for Beethoven to study with the old master.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200841_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200841-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Establishing_his_career_in_Vienna">Establishing his career in Vienna</span></h3>
<p>With the Elector's help, he left Bonn for Vienna in November 1792, amid rumours of <a href="/wiki/First_Coalition" class="mw-redirect" title="First Coalition">war spilling out of France</a>; he learned shortly after his arrival that his father had died.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer1921124_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer1921124-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer1921148_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer1921148-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200842_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200842-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Mozart had also recently died. Count Waldstein, in his farewell note to Beethoven, wrote: "Through uninterrupted diligence you will receive Mozart's spirit through Haydn's hands."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200842_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200842-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Over the next few years, Beethoven responded to the widespread feeling that he was a successor to the recently deceased Mozart by studying that master's work and writing works with a distinctly Mozartean flavour.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200843_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200843-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Beethoven_Riedel_1801.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Beethoven_Riedel_1801.jpg/170px-Beethoven_Riedel_1801.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="193" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Beethoven_Riedel_1801.jpg/255px-Beethoven_Riedel_1801.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Beethoven_Riedel_1801.jpg/340px-Beethoven_Riedel_1801.jpg 2x" data-file-width="425" data-file-height="482" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Beethoven_Riedel_1801.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait of Beethoven as a young man by Carl Traugott Riedel (1769–1832)</div></div></div>
<p>He did not immediately set out to establish himself as a composer, but rather devoted himself to study and performance. Working under Haydn's direction,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroveOnlinesection_3_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroveOnlinesection_3-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> he sought to master <a href="/wiki/Counterpoint" title="Counterpoint">counterpoint</a>. He also studied violin under <a href="/wiki/Ignaz_Schuppanzigh" title="Ignaz Schuppanzigh">Ignaz Schuppanzigh</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200847,_54_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200847,_54-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Early in this period, he also began receiving occasional instruction from <a href="/wiki/Antonio_Salieri" title="Antonio Salieri">Antonio Salieri</a>, primarily in Italian vocal composition style; this relationship persisted until at least 1802, and possibly as late as 1809.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer1921161_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer1921161-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> With Haydn's departure for England in 1794, Beethoven was expected by the Elector to return home to Bonn. He chose instead to remain in Vienna, continuing his instruction in counterpoint with <a href="/wiki/Johann_Georg_Albrechtsberger" title="Johann Georg Albrechtsberger">Johann Albrechtsberger</a> and other teachers. In any case, by this time it must have seemed clear to his employer that Bonn would fall to the French, as it did in October 1794, effectively leaving Beethoven without a stipend or the necessity to return.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> However, a number of Viennese noblemen had already recognised his ability and offered him financial support, among them <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Franz_von_Lobkowitz" title="Joseph Franz von Lobkowitz">Prince Joseph Franz Lobkowitz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Karl_Alois_Johann-Nepomuk_Vinzenz,_F%C3%BCrst_Lichnowsky" class="mw-redirect" title="Karl Alois Johann-Nepomuk Vinzenz, Fürst Lichnowsky">Prince Karl Lichnowsky</a>, and Baron <a href="/wiki/Gottfried_van_Swieten" title="Gottfried van Swieten">Gottfried van Swieten</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200853_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200853-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>By 1793, he had established a reputation as an improviser in the salons of the nobility, often playing the <a href="/wiki/Prelude_(music)" title="Prelude (music)">preludes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fugue" title="Fugue">fugues</a> of <a href="/wiki/Johann_Sebastian_Bach" title="Johann Sebastian Bach">J.S. Bach</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Well-Tempered_Clavier" class="mw-redirect" title="Well-Tempered Clavier">Well-Tempered Clavier</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Cross59_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cross59-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> His friend <a href="/wiki/Nikolaus_Simrock" title="Nikolaus Simrock">Nikolaus Simrock</a> had begun publishing his compositions; the first are believed to be a set of variations (WoO 66).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200846_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200846-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> By 1793, he had established a reputation in Vienna as a piano virtuoso, but he apparently withheld works from publication so that their publication in 1795 would have greater impact.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200853_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200853-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> His first public performance in Vienna was in March 1795, a concert in which he first performed one of his <a href="/wiki/Piano_concerto" title="Piano concerto">piano concertos</a>. It is uncertain whether this was the <a href="/wiki/Piano_Concerto_No._1_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Concerto No. 1 (Beethoven)">First</a> or <a href="/wiki/Piano_Concerto_No._2_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Concerto No. 2 (Beethoven)">Second</a>. Documentary evidence is unclear, and both concertos were in a similar state of near-completion (neither was completed or published for several years).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200859_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200859-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELockwood2005144_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELockwood2005144-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Shortly after this performance, he arranged for the publication of the first of his compositions to which he assigned an <a href="/wiki/Opus_number" title="Opus number">opus number</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Piano_Trios,_Op._1_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Trios, Op. 1 (Beethoven)">three piano trios, Opus 1</a>. These works were dedicated to his patron Prince Lichnowsky,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200859_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200859-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> and were a financial success; Beethoven's profits were nearly sufficient to cover his living expenses for a year.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200856_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200856-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Musical_maturation">Musical maturation</span></h3>
<p>Beethoven composed his <a href="/wiki/String_Quartets_Nos._1%E2%80%936,_Op._18_(Beethoven)" title="String Quartets Nos. 1–6, Op. 18 (Beethoven)">first six string quartets (Op. 18)</a> between 1798 and 1800 (commissioned by, and dedicated to, Prince Lobkowitz). They were published in 1801. With premieres of his <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._1_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 1 (Beethoven)">First</a> and <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._2_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 2 (Beethoven)">Second</a> Symphonies in 1800 and 1803, he became regarded as one of the most important of a generation of young composers following Haydn and Mozart. He also continued to write in other forms, turning out widely known <a href="/wiki/Piano_sonata" title="Piano sonata">piano sonatas</a> like the "<a href="/wiki/Piano_Sonata_No._8_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Sonata No. 8 (Beethoven)">Pathétique</a>" sonata (Op. 13), which Cooper describes as "surpass[ing] any of his previous compositions, in strength of character, depth of emotion, level of originality, and ingenuity of motivic and tonal manipulation."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200882_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200882-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> He also completed his <a href="/wiki/Septet_(Beethoven)" title="Septet (Beethoven)">Septet</a> (Op. 20) in 1799, which was one of his most popular works during his lifetime.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Beethoven_Hornemann.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Beethoven_Hornemann.jpg/170px-Beethoven_Hornemann.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="194" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Beethoven_Hornemann.jpg/255px-Beethoven_Hornemann.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Beethoven_Hornemann.jpg/340px-Beethoven_Hornemann.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="912" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Beethoven_Hornemann.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Beethoven in 1803, painted by <a href="/wiki/Christian_Horneman" title="Christian Horneman">Christian Horneman</a></div></div></div>
<p>For the premiere of his <i>First Symphony</i>, he hired the <a href="/wiki/Burgtheater" title="Burgtheater">Burgtheater</a> on 2&#160;April 1800, and staged an extensive programme of music, including works by Haydn and Mozart, as well as his Septet, the First Symphony, and one of his piano concertos (the latter three works all then unpublished). The concert, which the <i><a href="/wiki/Allgemeine_musikalische_Zeitung" title="Allgemeine musikalische Zeitung">Allgemeine musikalische Zeitung</a></i> described as "the most interesting concert in a long time," was not without difficulties; among the criticisms was that "the players did not bother to pay any attention to the soloist."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200890_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200890-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Mozart and Haydn were undeniable influences. For example, Beethoven's <a href="/wiki/Quintet_for_Piano_and_Winds_(Beethoven)" title="Quintet for Piano and Winds (Beethoven)">quintet for piano and winds</a> is said to bear a strong resemblance to <a href="/wiki/Quintet_for_Piano_and_Winds_(Mozart)" title="Quintet for Piano and Winds (Mozart)">Mozart's work for the same configuration</a>, albeit with his own distinctive touches.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200866_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200866-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
But his melodies, musical development, use of modulation and texture, and characterisation of emotion all set him apart from his influences, and heightened the impact some of his early works made when they were first published.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200858_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200858-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> By the end of 1800, Beethoven and his music were already much in demand from patrons and publishers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200897_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200897-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In May 1799, he taught piano to the daughters of Hungarian Countess Anna Brunsvik. During this time, he fell in love with the younger daughter <a href="/wiki/Josephine_Brunsvik" title="Josephine Brunsvik">Josephine</a><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> who has therefore been identified as one of the more likely candidates for the addressee of his letter to the "<a href="/wiki/Immortal_Beloved" title="Immortal Beloved">Immortal Beloved</a>" (in 1812). Shortly after these lessons, Josephine was married to Count Josef Deym. Beethoven was a regular visitor at their house, continuing to teach Josephine, and playing at parties and concerts. Her marriage was by all accounts happy (despite initial financial problems),<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> and the couple had four children. Her relationship with Beethoven intensified after Deym died suddenly in 1804.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200880_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200880-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Beethoven_3.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Beethoven_3.jpg/170px-Beethoven_3.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="202" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Beethoven_3.jpg/255px-Beethoven_3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Beethoven_3.jpg/340px-Beethoven_3.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1276" data-file-height="1515" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Beethoven_3.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ludwig van Beethoven: detail of an <a href="/wiki/Beethoven_(M%C3%A4hler,_1804%E2%80%9305)" title="Beethoven (Mähler, 1804–05)">1804–05 portrait</a> by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Willibrord_M%C3%A4hler" title="Joseph Willibrord Mähler">Joseph Willibrord Mähler</a>. The <a class="external text" href="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0b/Beethoven-M%C3%A4hler_1804_hires.jpg">complete painting</a> depicts Beethoven with a <a href="/wiki/Lyre-guitar" title="Lyre-guitar">lyre-guitar</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>He also had a few other students. From 1801 to 1805, he tutored <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Ries" title="Ferdinand Ries">Ferdinand Ries</a>, who went on to become a composer and later wrote <i>Beethoven remembered</i>, a book about their encounters. The young <a href="/wiki/Carl_Czerny" title="Carl Czerny">Carl Czerny</a> studied with Beethoven from 1801 to 1803. Czerny later became a renowned music teacher himself, instructing <a href="/wiki/Franz_Liszt" title="Franz Liszt">Franz Liszt</a>, and on 11 February 1812 gave the Vienna premiere of Beethoven's <a href="/wiki/Piano_Concerto_No._5_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Concerto No. 5 (Beethoven)">fifth piano concerto (the "Emperor")</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer1921526_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer1921526-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>His compositions between 1800 and 1802 were dominated by two large-scale orchestral works, although he continued to produce other important works such as the piano sonata <a href="/wiki/Piano_Sonata_No._14_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Sonata No. 14 (Beethoven)">Sonata quasi una fantasia</a> known as the "Moonlight Sonata". In the spring of 1801 he completed <i><a href="/wiki/The_Creatures_of_Prometheus" title="The Creatures of Prometheus">The Creatures of Prometheus</a></i>, a <a href="/wiki/Ballet" title="Ballet">ballet</a>. The work received numerous performances in 1801 and 1802, and he rushed to publish a piano arrangement to capitalise on its early popularity.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200898–103_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200898–103-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> In the spring of 1802 he completed the <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._2_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 2 (Beethoven)">Second Symphony</a>, intended for performance at a concert that was cancelled. The symphony received its premiere instead at a subscription concert in April 1803 at the <a href="/wiki/Theater_an_der_Wien" title="Theater an der Wien">Theater an der Wien</a>, where he had been appointed composer in residence. In addition to the Second Symphony, the concert also featured the First Symphony, the <a href="/wiki/Piano_Concerto_No._3_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Concerto No. 3 (Beethoven)">Third Piano Concerto</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Oratorio" title="Oratorio">oratorio</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Christ_on_the_Mount_of_Olives_(Beethoven)" title="Christ on the Mount of Olives (Beethoven)">Christ on the Mount of Olives</a></i>. Reviews were mixed, but the concert was a financial success; he was able to charge three times the cost of a typical concert ticket.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper2008112–27_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper2008112–27-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>His business dealings with publishers also began to improve in 1802 when his brother Kaspar, who had previously assisted him casually, began to assume a larger role in the management of his affairs. In addition to negotiating higher prices for recently composed works, Kaspar also began selling some of his earlier unpublished compositions, and encouraged him (against Beethoven's preference) to also make arrangements and transcriptions of his more popular works for other instrument combinations. Beethoven acceded to these requests, as he could not prevent publishers from hiring others to do similar arrangements of his works.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper2008112–15_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper2008112–15-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Loss_of_hearing">Loss of hearing</span></h3>
<p>Beethoven is reported to have dated his hearing loss from a fit he suffered in 1798 induced by a rage at the interruption of his work—having fallen over, he got up to find himself deaf. His hearing only ever partially recovered and, during its gradual decline, was impeded by a severe form of <a href="/wiki/Tinnitus" title="Tinnitus">tinnitus</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESwafford2014223–24_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESwafford2014223–24-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> As early as 1801, he wrote to friends describing his symptoms and the difficulties they caused in both professional and social settings (although it is likely some of his close friends were already aware of the problems).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper2008108_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper2008108-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The cause of his deafness is unknown, but has variously been attributed to <a href="/wiki/Typhus" title="Typhus">typhus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Auto-immune_disorder" class="mw-redirect" title="Auto-immune disorder">auto-immune disorders</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Systemic_lupus_erythematosus" title="Systemic lupus erythematosus">systemic lupus erythematosus</a>), and even his habit of immersing his head in cold water to stay awake.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> The explanation from his autopsy was that he had a "distended inner ear", which developed lesions over time.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
<a href="/wiki/Paget%27s_disease_of_bone" title="Paget&#39;s disease of bone">Paget's disease</a> is another possible cause of his deafness.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Beethoven_M%C3%A4hler_1815.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Beethoven_M%C3%A4hler_1815.jpg/170px-Beethoven_M%C3%A4hler_1815.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="215" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Beethoven_M%C3%A4hler_1815.jpg/255px-Beethoven_M%C3%A4hler_1815.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Beethoven_M%C3%A4hler_1815.jpg/340px-Beethoven_M%C3%A4hler_1815.jpg 2x" data-file-width="775" data-file-height="980" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Beethoven_M%C3%A4hler_1815.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Beethoven in 1815 portrait by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Willibrord_M%C3%A4hler" title="Joseph Willibrord Mähler">Joseph Willibrord Mähler</a></div></div></div>
<p>On the advice of his doctor, he lived in the small Austrian town of <a href="/wiki/Heiligenstadt,_Vienna" title="Heiligenstadt, Vienna">Heiligenstadt</a>, just outside Vienna, from April to October 1802 in an attempt to come to terms with his condition. There he wrote his <a href="/wiki/Heiligenstadt_Testament" title="Heiligenstadt Testament">Heiligenstadt Testament</a>, a letter to his brothers which records his thoughts of suicide due to his growing deafness and records his resolution to continue living for and through his art.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper2008120_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper2008120-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> Over time, his hearing loss became profound: at the end of the premiere of his <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._9_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 9 (Beethoven)">Ninth Symphony</a> in 1824, he had to be turned around to see the tumultuous applause of the audience because he could hear neither it nor the orchestra. His hearing loss did not prevent him from composing music, but it made playing at concerts—a lucrative source of income—increasingly difficult. After a failed attempt in 1811 to perform his own <a href="/wiki/Piano_Concerto_No._5_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Concerto No. 5 (Beethoven)">Piano Concerto No. 5 (the "Emperor")</a>, which was premiered by his student <a href="/wiki/Carl_Czerny" title="Carl Czerny">Carl Czerny</a>, he never performed in public again until he directed the premiere performance of the Ninth Symphony in 1824, which involved him giving cues to conductor <a href="/wiki/Michael_Umlauf" title="Michael Umlauf">Michael Umlauf</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A large collection of his hearing aids, such as a special <a href="/wiki/Ear_horn" class="mw-redirect" title="Ear horn">ear horn</a>, can be viewed at the <a href="/wiki/Beethoven_House" title="Beethoven House">Beethoven House</a> Museum in Bonn, Germany. Despite his obvious distress, Czerny remarked that Beethoven could still hear speech and music normally until 1812.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Around 1814 however, by the age of 44, he was almost totally deaf, and when a group of visitors saw him play a loud arpeggio of thundering bass notes at his piano remarking, "Ist es nicht schön?" (Is it not beautiful?), they felt deep sympathy considering his courage and sense of humour (he lost the ability to hear higher frequencies first).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolomon2000_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolomon2000-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As a result of Beethoven's hearing loss, his conversation books are an unusually rich written resource. Used primarily in the last ten or so years of his life, his friends wrote in these books so that he could know what they were saying, and he then responded either orally or in the book. The books contain discussions about music and other matters, and give insights into his thinking; they are a source for investigations into how he intended his music should be performed, and also his perception of his relationship to art. Out of a total of 400 conversation books, it has been suggested by <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Wheelock_Thayer" title="Alexander Wheelock Thayer">Alexander Wheelock Thayer</a> that 264 were destroyed (and others were altered) after his death by his secretary <a href="/wiki/Anton_Schindler" title="Anton Schindler">Anton Schindler</a>, who wished only an idealised biography of the composer to survive.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClive2001239_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClive2001239-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> However, <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Albrecht" title="Theodore Albrecht">Theodore Albrecht</a> contests the verity of Schindler's destruction of a large number of conversation books.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Patronage">Patronage</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rudolf-habsburg-olmuetz.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Rudolf-habsburg-olmuetz.jpg/170px-Rudolf-habsburg-olmuetz.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="226" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Rudolf-habsburg-olmuetz.jpg/255px-Rudolf-habsburg-olmuetz.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Rudolf-habsburg-olmuetz.jpg/340px-Rudolf-habsburg-olmuetz.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1378" data-file-height="1829" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rudolf-habsburg-olmuetz.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Beethoven's patron, <a href="/wiki/Archduke_Rudolf_of_Austria_(1788%E2%80%931831)" title="Archduke Rudolf of Austria (1788–1831)">Archduke Rudolph</a></div></div></div>
<p>While Beethoven earned income from publication of his works and from public performances, he also depended on the generosity of patrons for income, for whom he gave private performances and copies of works they commissioned for an exclusive period prior to their publication. Some of his early patrons, including Prince Lobkowitz and Prince Lichnowsky, gave him annual stipends in addition to commissioning works and purchasing published works.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200878–79_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200878–79-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Perhaps his most important aristocratic patron was <a href="/wiki/Archduke_Rudolf_of_Austria_(1788%E2%80%931831)" title="Archduke Rudolf of Austria (1788–1831)">Archduke Rudolph</a>, the youngest son of <a href="/wiki/Leopold_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Leopold II, Holy Roman Emperor">Emperor Leopold II</a>, who in 1803 or 1804 began to study piano and composition with him. The cleric (<a href="/wiki/Cardinal-Priest" class="mw-redirect" title="Cardinal-Priest">Cardinal-Priest</a>) and the composer became friends, and their meetings continued until 1824.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELockwood2005300–01_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELockwood2005300–01-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Beethoven dedicated 14 compositions to Rudolph, including the <a href="/wiki/Piano_Trio_No._7_(Beethoven)" class="mw-redirect" title="Piano Trio No. 7 (Beethoven)">Archduke Trio</a> (1811) and <i><a href="/wiki/Missa_solemnis_(Beethoven)" title="Missa solemnis (Beethoven)">Missa solemnis</a></i> (1823). Rudolph, in turn, dedicated one of his own compositions to Beethoven. The letters Beethoven wrote to Rudolph are today kept at the <a href="/wiki/Gesellschaft_der_Musikfreunde" title="Gesellschaft der Musikfreunde">Gesellschaft der Musikfreunde</a> in Vienna.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> Another patron was Count (later Prince) <a href="/wiki/Andrey_Kirillovich_Razumovsky" class="mw-redirect" title="Andrey Kirillovich Razumovsky">Andreas Razumovsky</a>, for whom the <a href="/wiki/String_Quartets_Nos._7%E2%80%939,_Op._59_%E2%80%93_Rasumovsky_(Beethoven)" title="String Quartets Nos. 7–9, Op. 59 – Rasumovsky (Beethoven)">String Quartets Nos. 7–9, Op. 59, <i>Rasumovsky</i></a> were named.
</p><p>In the autumn of 1808, after having been rejected for a position at the royal theatre, he received an offer from <a href="/wiki/Napoleon_I_of_France" class="mw-redirect" title="Napoleon I of France">Napoleon</a>'s brother <a href="/wiki/J%C3%A9r%C3%B4me_Bonaparte" title="Jérôme Bonaparte">Jérôme Bonaparte</a>, then king of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Westphalia" title="Kingdom of Westphalia">Westphalia</a>, for a well-paid position as <a href="/wiki/Kapellmeister" title="Kapellmeister">Kapellmeister</a> at the court in <a href="/wiki/Kassel" title="Kassel">Cassel</a>. To persuade him to stay in Vienna, the Archduke Rudolph, <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand,_5th_Prince_Kinsky_of_Wchinitz_and_Tettau" title="Ferdinand, 5th Prince Kinsky of Wchinitz and Tettau">Prince Kinsky</a> and Prince Lobkowitz, after receiving representations from the composer's friends, pledged to pay him a pension of 4000 florins a year. Only Archduke Rudolph paid his share of the pension on the agreed date.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper2008195_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper2008195-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> Kinsky, immediately called to military duty, did not contribute and soon died after falling from his horse. Lobkowitz stopped paying in September 1811. No successors came forward to continue the patronage, and Beethoven relied mostly on selling composition rights and a small pension after 1815. The effects of these financial arrangements were undermined to some extent by <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">war with France</a>, which caused significant inflation when the government printed money to fund its war efforts.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_period">Middle period</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Beethoven_monument_bonn_muensterplatz_2008.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Beethoven_monument_bonn_muensterplatz_2008.jpg/170px-Beethoven_monument_bonn_muensterplatz_2008.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="361" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Beethoven_monument_bonn_muensterplatz_2008.jpg/255px-Beethoven_monument_bonn_muensterplatz_2008.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Beethoven_monument_bonn_muensterplatz_2008.jpg/340px-Beethoven_monument_bonn_muensterplatz_2008.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1790" data-file-height="3796" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Beethoven_monument_bonn_muensterplatz_2008.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Beethoven_Monument_(Bonn)" title="Beethoven Monument (Bonn)">Beethoven Monument</a> in Bonn, Münsterplatz</div></div></div>
<p>Beethoven's middle period spanned from 1802 through 1812.<sup id="cite_ref-mprbirthday_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mprbirthday-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>His return to Vienna from Heiligenstadt was marked by a change in musical style, and is now designated as the start of his middle or "heroic" period. According to Carl Czerny, Beethoven said, "I am not satisfied with the work I have done so far. From now on I intend to take a new way."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper2008131_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper2008131-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> This "heroic" phase was characterised by a large number of original works composed on a grand scale.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> The first major work employing this new style was the <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._3_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 3 (Beethoven)">Third Symphony</a> in E flat, known as the <i>Eroica</i>. This work was longer and larger in scope than any previous symphony. When it premiered in early 1805 it received a mixed reception. Some listeners objected to its length or misunderstood its structure, while others viewed it as a masterpiece.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper2008148_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper2008148-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The "middle period" is sometimes associated with a "heroic" manner of composing,<sup id="cite_ref-Solomon1990_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Solomon1990-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> but the use of the term "heroic" has become increasingly controversial in Beethoven scholarship. The term is more frequently used as an alternative name for the middle period.<sup id="cite_ref-Steinberg2006_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steinberg2006-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> The appropriateness of the term "heroic" to describe the whole middle period has been questioned as well: while some works, like the Third and Fifth Symphonies, are easy to describe as "heroic", many others, like his <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._6_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 6 (Beethoven)">Symphony No. 6, <i>Pastoral</i></a>, are not.<sup id="cite_ref-BurnhamSteinberg2000_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BurnhamSteinberg2000-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<table role="presentation" class="mbox-small noprint" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;">
<tbody><tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="50" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="160" data-file-height="160" /></div></div></td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em"><div class="haudio">
<div style="padding:4px 0"><a href="/wiki/File:Ludwig_van_Beethoven_-_Symphonie_5_c-moll_-_1._Allegro_con_brio.ogg" title="File:Ludwig van Beethoven - Symphonie 5 c-moll - 1. Allegro con brio.ogg">Symphony No. 5, Op. 67 (1st movement)</a></div>
<div><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_0" controls="" preload="none" style="width:220px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="436.02222222222" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Ludwig_van_Beethoven_-_Symphonie_5_c-moll_-_1._Allegro_con_brio.ogg" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5b/Ludwig_van_Beethoven_-_Symphonie_5_c-moll_-_1._Allegro_con_brio.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file (133 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="132877" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/5/5b/Ludwig_van_Beethoven_-_Symphonie_5_c-moll_-_1._Allegro_con_brio.ogg/Ludwig_van_Beethoven_-_Symphonie_5_c-moll_-_1._Allegro_con_brio.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-title="MP3" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="197584" /></audio></div></div>
<div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0">composed during Beethoven's middle period</div></div></td></tr>
<tr><td colspan="2" class="mbox-text" style="line-height:1.1em"><hr /><i class="selfreference">Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Help:Media" title="Help:Media">media help</a>.</i></td></tr>
</tbody></table>
<p>Some of the middle period works extend the musical language he had inherited from Haydn and Mozart. The middle period work includes the Third through <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._8_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 8 (Beethoven)">Eighth</a> Symphonies, the <a href="/wiki/String_Quartets_Nos._7%E2%80%939,_Op._59_%E2%80%93_Rasumovsky_(Beethoven)" title="String Quartets Nos. 7–9, Op. 59 – Rasumovsky (Beethoven)">Rasumovsky</a>, <a href="/wiki/String_Quartet_No._10_(Beethoven)" title="String Quartet No. 10 (Beethoven)"><i>Harp</i></a> and <a href="/wiki/String_Quartet_No._11_(Beethoven)" title="String Quartet No. 11 (Beethoven)"><i>Serioso</i></a> string quartets, the <i><a href="/wiki/Piano_Sonata_No._21_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Sonata No. 21 (Beethoven)">Waldstein</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Piano_Sonata_No._23_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Sonata No. 23 (Beethoven)">Appassionata</a></i> piano sonatas, <i>Christ on the Mount of Olives</i>, the opera <i><a href="/wiki/Fidelio" title="Fidelio">Fidelio</a></i>, the <a href="/wiki/Violin_Concerto_(Beethoven)" title="Violin Concerto (Beethoven)">Violin Concerto</a>, and many other compositions. A minor work, his 1815 <a href="/wiki/Finale_(music)" title="Finale (music)">finale</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Es_ist_vollbracht" title="Es ist vollbracht">Es ist vollbracht</a></i>, reflected the heroic style in an exaggerated fashion.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During this time his income came from publishing his works, from performances of them, and from his patrons. His position at the Theater an der Wien was terminated when the theatre changed management in early 1804, and he was forced to move temporarily to the suburbs of Vienna with his friend Stephan von Breuning. This slowed work on <i>Fidelio</i>, his largest work to date, for a time. It was delayed again by the Austrian <a href="/wiki/Censorship" title="Censorship">censor</a>, and finally premiered in November 1805 to houses that were nearly empty because of the <a href="/wiki/War_of_the_Third_Coalition" title="War of the Third Coalition">French occupation of the city</a>. In addition to being a financial failure, this version of <i>Fidelio</i> was also a critical failure, and Beethoven began revising it.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper2008150_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper2008150-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In May 1809, when the attacking forces of <a href="/wiki/Napoleon" title="Napoleon">Napoleon</a> <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Wagram#Initial_hostilities" title="Battle of Wagram">bombarded Vienna</a>, according to Ferdinand Ries, Beethoven, very worried that the noise would destroy what remained of his hearing, hid in the basement of his brother's house, covering his ears with pillows.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper2008185_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper2008185-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The work of the middle period established Beethoven as a master. In a review from 1810, he was enshrined by <a href="/wiki/E._T._A._Hoffmann" title="E. T. A. Hoffmann">E. T. A. Hoffmann</a> as one of the three great "<a href="/wiki/Romantic_music" title="Romantic music">Romantic</a>" composers, along with Haydn and Mozart; Hoffmann called his <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._5_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 5 (Beethoven)">Fifth Symphony</a> "one of the most important works of the age".<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_and_family_difficulties">Personal and family difficulties</span></h3>
<table role="presentation" class="mbox-small noprint" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;">
<tbody><tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="50" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="160" data-file-height="160" /></div></div></td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em"><div class="haudio">
<div style="padding:4px 0"><a href="/wiki/File:FurElise.ogg" title="File:FurElise.ogg">Für Elise</a></div>
<div><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_1" controls="" preload="none" style="width:220px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="176.58675736961" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="FurElise.ogg" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/7b/FurElise.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file (97 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="97135" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/7/7b/FurElise.ogg/FurElise.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-title="MP3" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="158528" /></audio></div></div>
<div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0"></div></div></td></tr>
<tr><td colspan="2" class="mbox-text" style="line-height:1.1em"><hr /><i class="selfreference">Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Help:Media" title="Help:Media">media help</a>.</i></td></tr>
</tbody></table>
<p>Beethoven's love life was hampered by class issues. In late 1801, he met a young countess, <a href="/wiki/Giulietta_Guicciardi" title="Giulietta Guicciardi">Julie ("Giulietta") Guicciardi</a>, through the Brunsvik family, at a time when he was giving regular piano lessons to <a href="/wiki/Josephine_Brunsvik" title="Josephine Brunsvik">Josephine Brunsvik</a>. He mentions his love for Julie in a November 1801 letter to his boyhood friend, Franz Wegeler, but he could not consider marrying her, due to the class difference. He later dedicated his <a href="/wiki/Piano_Sonata_No._14_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Sonata No. 14 (Beethoven)">Sonata No. 14</a>, now commonly known as the <i>Moonlight sonata</i>, to her.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESteblin2009_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESteblin2009-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>His relationship with Josephine Brunsvik deepened after the death in 1804 of her aristocratic first husband, the Count Joseph Deym.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>143</span></sup> He wrote Josephine 15 passionate love letters from late 1804 to around 1809/10. Although his feelings were obviously reciprocated, Josephine was forced by her family to withdraw from him in 1807. She cited her "duty" and the fact that she would have lost the custodianship of her aristocratic children had she married a commoner.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper2008146,_168_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper2008146,_168-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> After Josephine married Baron von Stackelberg in 1810, Beethoven may have proposed unsuccessfully to <a href="/wiki/Therese_Malfatti" title="Therese Malfatti">Therese Malfatti</a>, the supposed dedicatee of "<a href="/wiki/F%C3%BCr_Elise" title="Für Elise">Für Elise</a>";<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELorenz2011_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELorenz2011-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESteblin2014_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESteblin2014-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> his status as a commoner may again have interfered with those plans.
</p><p>In the spring of 1811 he became seriously ill, suffering headaches and high fever. On the advice of his doctor, he spent six weeks in the <a href="/wiki/Bohemia" title="Bohemia">Bohemian</a> <a href="/wiki/Spa_town" title="Spa town">spa town</a> of <a href="/wiki/Teplice" title="Teplice">Teplitz</a>. The following winter, which was dominated by work on the Seventh symphony, he was again ill, and his doctor ordered him to spend the summer of 1812 at the spa Teplitz. It is certain that he was at Teplitz when he wrote a love letter to his "<a href="/wiki/Immortal_Beloved" title="Immortal Beloved">Immortal Beloved</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrandenburg1996582_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrandenburg1996582-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> The identity of the intended recipient has long been a subject of debate; candidates include Guicciardi, Malfatti, Brunsvik, <a href="/wiki/Anna_Maria_Erd%C5%91dy" title="Anna Maria Erdődy">Anna Maria Erdődy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antonie_Brentano" title="Antonie Brentano">Antonie Brentano</a>.
</p><p>He visited his brother Johann at the end of October 1812. He wished to end Johann's cohabitation with Therese Obermayer, a woman who already had an illegitimate child. He was unable to convince Johann to end the relationship and appealed to the local civic and religious authorities. Johann and Therese married on 9&#160;November.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper2008212_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper2008212-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In early 1813 he apparently went through a difficult emotional period, and his compositional output dropped. His personal appearance degraded—it had generally been neat—as did his manners in public, especially when dining. He took care of his brother (who was suffering from tuberculosis) and his family, an expense that he claimed left him penniless.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Beethoven_Letronne.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Beethoven_Letronne.jpg/170px-Beethoven_Letronne.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="201" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Beethoven_Letronne.jpg/255px-Beethoven_Letronne.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Beethoven_Letronne.jpg/340px-Beethoven_Letronne.jpg 2x" data-file-width="493" data-file-height="583" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Beethoven_Letronne.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Beethoven in 1814; portrait by <a href="/w/index.php?title=Louis-Ren%C3%A9_Letronne&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Louis-René Letronne (page does not exist)">Louis-René Letronne</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&#160;&#91;<a href="https://fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Louis-Ren%C3%A9_Letronne" class="extiw" title="fr:Louis-René Letronne">fr</a>&#93;</span></div></div></div>
<p>Beethoven was finally motivated to begin significant composition again in June 1813, when news arrived of the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Vitoria" title="Battle of Vitoria">defeat of one of Napoleon's armies</a> at <a href="/wiki/Vitoria,_Spain" class="mw-redirect" title="Vitoria, Spain">Vitoria, Spain</a>, by a coalition of forces under the <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Wellesley,_1st_Duke_of_Wellington" title="Arthur Wellesley, 1st Duke of Wellington">Duke of Wellington</a>. This news stimulated him to write the battle symphony known as <i><a href="/wiki/Wellington%27s_Victory" title="Wellington&#39;s Victory">Wellington's Victory</a></i>. It was first performed on 8&#160;December, along with his Seventh Symphony, at a charity concert for victims of the war. The work was a popular hit, probably because of its programmatic style, which was entertaining and easy to understand. It received repeat performances at concerts he staged in January and February 1814. His renewed popularity led to demands for a revival of <i>Fidelio</i>, which, in its third revised version, was also well received at its July opening. That summer he composed a piano sonata for the first time in five years (<i><a href="/wiki/Piano_Sonata_No._27_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Sonata No. 27 (Beethoven)">No. 27, Opus 90</a></i>). This work was in a markedly more <a href="/wiki/Romanticism" title="Romanticism">Romantic</a> style than his earlier sonatas. He was also one of many composers who produced music in a patriotic vein to entertain the many heads of state and diplomats who came to the <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_Vienna" title="Congress of Vienna">Congress of Vienna</a> that began in November 1814. His output of songs included his only <a href="/wiki/Song_cycle" title="Song cycle">song cycle</a>, "<a href="/wiki/An_die_ferne_Geliebte" title="An die ferne Geliebte">An die ferne Geliebte</a>," and the extraordinarily expressive second setting of the poem "An die Hoffnung" (Op. 94) in 1815. Compared to its first setting in 1805 (a gift for Josephine Brunsvik), it was "far more dramatic&#160;... The entire spirit is that of an operatic scena."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELockwood2005278_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELockwood2005278-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Custody_struggle_and_illness">Custody struggle and illness</span></h3>
<p>Between 1815 and 1817 Beethoven's output dropped again. He attributed part of this to a lengthy illness (he called it an "inflammatory fever") that he had for more than a year, starting in October 1816.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper2008254_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper2008254-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> Biographers have speculated on a variety of other reasons that also contributed to the decline, including the difficulties in the personal lives of his would-be paramours and the harsh censorship policies of the Austrian government. The illness and death of his brother Kaspar from <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">tuberculosis</a> may also have played a role.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Beethoven_7.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Beethoven_7.jpg/170px-Beethoven_7.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="247" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Beethoven_7.jpg/255px-Beethoven_7.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Beethoven_7.jpg/340px-Beethoven_7.jpg 2x" data-file-width="440" data-file-height="640" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Beethoven_7.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Beethoven in 1818 by <a href="/w/index.php?title=August_Kl%C3%B6ber&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="August Klöber (page does not exist)">August Klöber</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&#160;&#91;<a href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/August_von_Kloeber" class="extiw" title="de:August von Kloeber">de</a>&#93;</span></div></div></div>
<p>Kaspar had been ill for some time, and Beethoven spent a small fortune in 1815 on his care. After Kaspar died on 15&#160;November 1815, Beethoven immediately became embroiled in a protracted legal dispute with Kaspar's wife <a href="/wiki/Johanna_van_Beethoven" title="Johanna van Beethoven">Johanna</a> over custody of their son <a href="/w/index.php?title=Karl_van_Beethoven_(born_1806)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Karl van Beethoven (born 1806) (page does not exist)">Karl</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&#160;&#91;<a href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_van_Beethoven" class="extiw" title="de:Karl van Beethoven">de</a>&#93;</span>, then nine years old. Beethoven, who considered Johanna an unfit parent because of her morals (she had an illegitimate child by a different father before marrying Kaspar and had been convicted of theft) and financial management, had successfully applied to Kaspar to have himself named sole guardian of the boy. A late <a href="/wiki/Codicil_(will)" title="Codicil (will)">codicil</a> to Kaspar's will gave him and Johanna joint guardianship. While Beethoven was successful at having his nephew removed from her custody in February 1816, the case was not fully resolved until 1820, and he was frequently preoccupied by the demands of the litigation and seeing to the welfare of Karl, whom he first placed in a private school.
</p><p>The Austrian court system had one court for the <a href="/wiki/Nobility" title="Nobility">nobility</a> and members of the <a href="/wiki/Landtafel" class="mw-redirect" title="Landtafel">Landtafel</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Landrecht_(medieval)" title="Landrecht (medieval)">Landrechte</a>, and many other courts for commoners, among them the Civil Court of the Vienna Magistrate. Beethoven disguised the fact that the Dutch "<a href="/wiki/Van_(Dutch)" title="Van (Dutch)">van</a>" in his name did not denote nobility as does the German "<a href="/wiki/Von" title="Von">von</a>"<sup id="cite_ref-landrecht_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-landrecht-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> and his case was tried in the Landrechte. Owing to his influence with the court, Beethoven felt assured of the favourable outcome of being awarded sole guardianship.
