While giving evidence to the Landrechte, however, he was unable to prove being of noble birth<sup id="cite_ref-landrecht_90-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-landrecht-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> and as a consequence, on 18&#160;December 1818 the case was transferred to the Magistracy, where he lost sole guardianship.
</p><p>He appealed and regained custody. Johanna's appeal to the Emperor was not successful: the Emperor "washed his hands of the matter". During the years of custody that followed, Beethoven attempted to ensure that Karl lived to the highest moral standards. Beethoven had an overbearing manner and frequently interfered in his nephew's life. Karl attempted suicide on 31&#160;July 1826 by shooting himself in the head. He survived and was brought to his mother's house, where he recuperated. He and Beethoven were reconciled, but Karl insisted on joining the army and last saw Beethoven in January 1827.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Late_works">Late works</span></h3>
<p>Beethoven's late period spanned from 1812 to his death in 1827.<sup id="cite_ref-mprbirthday_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mprbirthday-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>He began a renewed study of older music, including works by <a href="/wiki/Johann_Sebastian_Bach" title="Johann Sebastian Bach">Johann Sebastian Bach</a> and <a href="/wiki/George_Frideric_Handel" title="George Frideric Handel">George Frideric Handel</a>, that were then being published in the first attempts at complete editions. He composed the overture <i><a href="/wiki/The_Consecration_of_the_House_(overture)" title="The Consecration of the House (overture)">The Consecration of the House</a></i>, which was the first work to attempt to incorporate these influences. A new style emerged, now called his "late period". He returned to the keyboard to compose his first piano sonatas in almost a decade: the works of the late period include the last five piano sonatas and the <i><a href="/wiki/Diabelli_Variations" title="Diabelli Variations">Diabelli Variations</a></i>, the last two sonatas for cello and piano, the late string quartets (see below), and two works for very large forces: the <i><a href="/wiki/Missa_Solemnis_(Beethoven)" class="mw-redirect" title="Missa Solemnis (Beethoven)">Missa Solemnis</a></i> and the <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._9_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 9 (Beethoven)">Ninth Symphony</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>By early 1818 his health had improved, and his nephew moved in with him in January. On the downside, his hearing had deteriorated to the point that conversation became difficult, necessitating the use of conversation books. His household management had also improved somewhat; Nanette Streicher, who had assisted in his care during his illness, continued to provide some support, and he finally found a skilled cook.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper2008260_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper2008260-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> His musical output in 1818 was still somewhat reduced, but included song collections and the <a href="/wiki/Piano_Sonata_No._29_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Sonata No. 29 (Beethoven)">"Hammerklavier" Sonata</a>, as well as sketches for two symphonies that eventually coalesced into the epic Ninth. In 1819 he was again preoccupied by the legal processes around Karl, and began work on the <i><a href="/wiki/Diabelli_Variations" title="Diabelli Variations">Diabelli Variations</a></i> and the <i>Missa Solemnis</i>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Beethoven_Waldmuller_1823.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Beethoven_Waldmuller_1823.jpg/220px-Beethoven_Waldmuller_1823.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="271" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Beethoven_Waldmuller_1823.jpg/330px-Beethoven_Waldmuller_1823.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/95/Beethoven_Waldmuller_1823.jpg 2x" data-file-width="389" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Beethoven_Waldmuller_1823.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Beethoven in 1823 by <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Georg_Waldm%C3%BCller" title="Ferdinand Georg Waldmüller">Ferdinand Georg Waldmüller</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>For the next few years he continued to work on the <i>Missa</i>, composing piano sonatas and <a href="/wiki/Bagatelle_(music)" title="Bagatelle (music)">bagatelles</a> to satisfy the demands of publishers and the need for income, and completing the <i>Diabelli Variations</i>. He was ill again for an extended time in 1821, and completed the <i>Missa</i> in 1823, three years after its original due date. He also opened discussions with his publishers over the possibility of producing a complete edition of his work, an idea that was arguably not fully realised until 1971.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> His brother Johann began to take a hand in his business affairs, much in the way Kaspar had earlier, locating older unpublished works to offer for publication and offering the <i>Missa</i> to multiple publishers with the goal of getting a higher price for it.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Two commissions in 1822 improved his financial prospects. The <a href="/wiki/Philharmonic_Society" class="mw-redirect" title="Philharmonic Society">Philharmonic Society</a> of London offered a commission for a symphony, and Prince <a href="/wiki/Nikolay_Borisovich_Galitzin" class="mw-redirect" title="Nikolay Borisovich Galitzin">Nikolas Golitsin</a> of <a href="/wiki/Saint_Petersburg" title="Saint Petersburg">St. Petersburg</a> offered to pay Beethoven's price for three string quartets. The first of these commissions spurred him to finish the Ninth Symphony, which was first performed, along with the <i>Missa Solemnis</i>, on 7&#160;May 1824, to great acclaim at the <a href="/wiki/K%C3%A4rntnertortheater" class="mw-redirect" title="Kärntnertortheater">Kärntnertortheater</a>. The <i>Allgemeine musikalische Zeitung</i> gushed, "inexhaustible genius had shown us a new world", and Carl Czerny wrote that his symphony "breathes such a fresh, lively, indeed youthful spirit&#160;... so much power, innovation, and beauty as ever [came] from the head of this original man, although he certainly sometimes led the old wigs to shake their heads." Unlike his more lucrative earlier concerts, this did not make him much money, as the expenses of mounting it were significantly higher.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper2008317_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper2008317-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> A second concert on 24&#160;May, in which the producer guaranteed him a minimum fee, was poorly attended; nephew Karl noted that "many people [had] already gone into the country". It was Beethoven's last public concert.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper2008318_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper2008318-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<table role="presentation" class="mbox-small noprint" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;">
<tbody><tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="50" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="160" data-file-height="160" /></div></div></td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em"><div class="haudio">
<div style="padding:4px 0"><a href="/wiki/File:Beethoven_-_Piano_sonata_in_C_minor_(opus_111),_movement_1.ogg" title="File:Beethoven - Piano sonata in C minor (opus 111), movement 1.ogg">Piano Sonata No. 32 in C minor, Op. 111 (1st movement)</a></div>
<div><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_2" controls="" preload="none" style="width:220px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="548.18394557823" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Beethoven_-_Piano_sonata_in_C_minor_(opus_111),_movement_1.ogg" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/95/Beethoven_-_Piano_sonata_in_C_minor_%28opus_111%29%2C_movement_1.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file (152 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="152214" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/9/95/Beethoven_-_Piano_sonata_in_C_minor_%28opus_111%29%2C_movement_1.ogg/Beethoven_-_Piano_sonata_in_C_minor_%28opus_111%29%2C_movement_1.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-title="MP3" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="210288" /></audio></div></div>
<div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0">written between 1821 and 1822, during Beethoven's late period</div></div></td></tr>
<tr><td colspan="2" class="mbox-text" style="line-height:1.1em"><hr /><i class="selfreference">Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Help:Media" title="Help:Media">media help</a>.</i></td></tr>
</tbody></table>
<p>Beethoven then turned to writing the string quartets for Golitsin. This series of quartets, known as the "<a href="/wiki/Late_String_Quartets_(Beethoven)" class="mw-redirect" title="Late String Quartets (Beethoven)">Late Quartets</a>," went far beyond what musicians or audiences were ready for at that time. Composer <a href="/wiki/Louis_Spohr" title="Louis Spohr">Louis Spohr</a> called them "indecipherable, uncorrected horrors." Opinion has changed considerably from the time of their first bewildered reception: their forms and ideas inspired musicians and composers including <a href="/wiki/Richard_Wagner" title="Richard Wagner">Richard Wagner</a> and <a href="/wiki/B%C3%A9la_Bart%C3%B3k" title="Béla Bartók">Béla Bartók</a>, and continue to do so. Of the late quartets, Beethoven's favourite was the <a href="/wiki/String_Quartet_No._14_(Beethoven)" title="String Quartet No. 14 (Beethoven)">Fourteenth Quartet, op. 131</a> in C<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-sharp">&#x266f;</span></span> minor, which he rated as his most perfect single work.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorris2010213_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorris2010213-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> The last musical wish of <a href="/wiki/Franz_Schubert" title="Franz Schubert">Schubert</a> was to hear the Op. 131 quartet, which he did on 14 November 1828, five days before his death.<sup id="cite_ref-Winter1994_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Winter1994-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>He wrote the last quartets amidst failing health. In April 1825 he was bedridden, and remained ill for about a month. The illness—or more precisely, his recovery from it—is remembered for having given rise to the deeply felt slow movement of the <a href="/wiki/String_Quartet_No._15_(Beethoven)" title="String Quartet No. 15 (Beethoven)">Fifteenth Quartet</a>, which he called "Holy song of thanks ('Heiliger Dankgesang') to the divinity, from one made well." He went on to complete the quartets now numbered <a href="/wiki/String_Quartet_No._13_(Beethoven)" title="String Quartet No. 13 (Beethoven)">Thirteenth</a>, <a href="/wiki/String_Quartet_No._14_(Beethoven)" title="String Quartet No. 14 (Beethoven)">Fourteenth</a>, and <a href="/wiki/String_Quartet_No._16_(Beethoven)" title="String Quartet No. 16 (Beethoven)">Sixteenth</a>. The last work completed by Beethoven was the substitute final movement of the Thirteenth Quartet, which replaced the difficult <i><a href="/wiki/Gro%C3%9Fe_Fuge" title="Große Fuge">Große Fuge</a></i>. Shortly thereafter, in December 1826, illness struck again, with episodes of vomiting and diarrhoea that nearly ended his life.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>In 1825, his nine symphonies were performed in a cycle for the first time, by the <a href="/wiki/Leipzig_Gewandhaus_Orchestra" title="Leipzig Gewandhaus Orchestra">Leipzig Gewandhaus Orchestra</a> under <a href="/wiki/Johann_Philipp_Christian_Schulz" title="Johann Philipp Christian Schulz">Johann Philipp Christian Schulz</a>. This was repeated in 1826.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Illness_and_death">Illness and death</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Death_of_Ludwig_van_Beethoven" title="Death of Ludwig van Beethoven">Death of Ludwig van Beethoven</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Zentralfriedhof_Vienna_-_Beethoven.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Zentralfriedhof_Vienna_-_Beethoven.JPG/170px-Zentralfriedhof_Vienna_-_Beethoven.JPG" decoding="async" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Zentralfriedhof_Vienna_-_Beethoven.JPG/255px-Zentralfriedhof_Vienna_-_Beethoven.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Zentralfriedhof_Vienna_-_Beethoven.JPG/340px-Zentralfriedhof_Vienna_-_Beethoven.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1920" data-file-height="2560" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Zentralfriedhof_Vienna_-_Beethoven.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Beethoven's grave site, Vienna <a href="/wiki/Zentralfriedhof" class="mw-redirect" title="Zentralfriedhof">Zentralfriedhof</a></div></div></div>
<p>Beethoven was bedridden for most of his remaining months, and many friends came to visit. He died on 26 March 1827 at the age of 56 during a thunderstorm. His friend <a href="/wiki/Anselm_H%C3%BCttenbrenner" title="Anselm Hüttenbrenner">Anselm Hüttenbrenner</a>, who was present at the time, said that there was a peal of thunder at the moment of death. An <a href="/wiki/Autopsy" title="Autopsy">autopsy</a> revealed significant <a href="/wiki/Liver" title="Liver">liver</a> damage, which may have been due to heavy alcohol consumption.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper2008318,_349_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper2008318,_349-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> It also revealed considerable dilation of the auditory and other related nerves.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Beethoven's funeral procession on 29&#160;March 1827 was attended by an estimated 20,000 people. <a href="/wiki/Franz_Schubert" title="Franz Schubert">Franz Schubert</a>, who died the following year and was buried next to him, was one of the torchbearers. He was buried in a dedicated grave in the <a href="/wiki/W%C3%A4hring" title="Währing">Währing</a> cemetery, north-west of Vienna, after a requiem mass at the church of the Holy Trinity (Dreifaltigkeitskirche). His remains were <a href="/wiki/Exhume" class="mw-redirect" title="Exhume">exhumed</a> for study in 1862, and moved in 1888 to Vienna's <a href="/wiki/Zentralfriedhof" class="mw-redirect" title="Zentralfriedhof">Zentralfriedhof</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper2008318,_349_99-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper2008318,_349-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> In 2012, his crypt was checked to see if his teeth had been stolen during a series of grave robberies of other famous Viennese composers.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There is dispute about the cause of his death: <a href="/wiki/Alcoholic_cirrhosis" class="mw-redirect" title="Alcoholic cirrhosis">alcoholic cirrhosis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syphilis" title="Syphilis">syphilis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Infectious_hepatitis" class="mw-redirect" title="Infectious hepatitis">infectious hepatitis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lead_poisoning" title="Lead poisoning">lead poisoning</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sarcoidosis" title="Sarcoidosis">sarcoidosis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Whipple%27s_disease" title="Whipple&#39;s disease">Whipple's disease</a> have all been proposed.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> Friends and visitors before and after his death clipped locks of his hair, some of which have been preserved and subjected to additional analysis, as have skull fragments removed during the 1862 <a href="/wiki/Exhumation" class="mw-redirect" title="Exhumation">exhumation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeredith20052–3_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeredith20052–3-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> Some of these analyses have led to controversial assertions that he was accidentally <a href="/wiki/Poison" title="Poison">poisoned</a> to death by excessive doses of lead-based treatments administered under instruction from his doctor.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Beethoven_bust_statue_by_Hagen.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Beethoven_bust_statue_by_Hagen.jpg/170px-Beethoven_bust_statue_by_Hagen.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="253" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Beethoven_bust_statue_by_Hagen.jpg/255px-Beethoven_bust_statue_by_Hagen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Beethoven_bust_statue_by_Hagen.jpg/340px-Beethoven_bust_statue_by_Hagen.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2350" data-file-height="3500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Beethoven_bust_statue_by_Hagen.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A bust by <a href="/wiki/Hugo_Hagen" title="Hugo Hagen">Hugo Hagen</a> based upon Beethoven's life mask</div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Beethoven%27s_musical_style" title="Beethoven&#39;s musical style">Beethoven's musical style</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beethoven_and_C_minor" title="Beethoven and C minor">Beethoven and C minor</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_compositions_by_Ludwig_van_Beethoven" title="List of compositions by Ludwig van Beethoven">List of compositions by Ludwig van Beethoven</a></div>
<p>Beethoven is acknowledged to be one of the giants of <a href="/wiki/Classical_music" title="Classical music">classical music</a>. Together with <a href="/wiki/Johann_Sebastian_Bach" title="Johann Sebastian Bach">Bach</a> and <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Brahms" title="Johannes Brahms">Johannes Brahms</a>, he is referred to as one of the "<a href="/wiki/Three_Bs" title="Three Bs">three <i>B</i>s</a>" who epitomize that tradition. He was a pivotal figure in the transition from the 18th century musical <a href="/wiki/Classical_music_era" class="mw-redirect" title="Classical music era">classicism</a> to 19th century <a href="/wiki/Romantic_music" title="Romantic music">romanticism</a>, and his influence on subsequent generations of composers was profound.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroveOnline_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroveOnline-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> His music features twice on the <a href="/wiki/Voyager_Golden_Record" title="Voyager Golden Record">Voyager Golden Record</a>, a phonograph record containing a broad sample of the images, common sounds, languages, and music of Earth, sent into outer space with the two <a href="/wiki/Voyager_program" title="Voyager program">Voyager</a> probes.<sup id="cite_ref-Golden_Record_Music_List_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Golden_Record_Music_List-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Overview">Overview</span></h3>
<p>Beethoven composed in several musical genres and for a variety of instrument combinations. His works for <a href="/wiki/Symphony_orchestra" class="mw-redirect" title="Symphony orchestra">symphony orchestra</a> include nine <a href="/wiki/Symphony" title="Symphony">symphonies</a> (of which the Ninth Symphony includes a chorus), and about a dozen pieces of "occasional" music. He wrote seven <a href="/wiki/Concerto" title="Concerto">concerti</a> for one or more soloists and orchestra, as well as four shorter works that include soloists accompanied by orchestra. His only <a href="/wiki/Opera" title="Opera">opera</a> is <i><a href="/wiki/Fidelio" title="Fidelio">Fidelio</a></i>; other vocal works with orchestral accompaniment include two <a href="/wiki/Mass_(music)" title="Mass (music)">masses</a> and a number of shorter works.
</p><p>His large body of compositions for <a href="/wiki/Piano" title="Piano">piano</a> includes 32 <a href="/wiki/Piano_sonata" title="Piano sonata">piano sonatas</a> and numerous shorter pieces, including arrangements of some of his other works. Works with piano accompaniment include 10 violin sonatas, 5 cello sonatas, and a sonata for <a href="/wiki/French_horn" title="French horn">French horn</a>, as well as numerous <a href="/wiki/Lieder" class="mw-redirect" title="Lieder">lieder</a>.
</p><p>He also wrote a significant quantity of <a href="/wiki/Chamber_music" title="Chamber music">chamber music</a>. In addition to 16 <a href="/wiki/String_quartet" title="String quartet">string quartets</a>, he wrote five works for <a href="/wiki/String_quintet" title="String quintet">string quintet</a>, seven for <a href="/wiki/Piano_trio" title="Piano trio">piano trio</a>, five for <a href="/wiki/String_trio" title="String trio">string trio</a>, and more than a dozen works for various combinations of <a href="/wiki/Wind_instrument" title="Wind instrument">wind instruments</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_three_periods">The three periods</span></h3>
<p>Beethoven's career as a composer is conventionally divided into early, middle, and late periods. The "early" period is typically seen to last until 1802, the "middle" period from 1802 to 1812, and the "late" period thereafter. This distinction was first introduced in 1828, just one year after his death, and while often challenged and refined it remains a starting point to understand the development of Beethoven's work.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroveOnlinesection_11_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroveOnlinesection_11-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Beethoven's early years in Bonn arguably represent a further, preliminary, period. His earliest known composition was from 1782, and a total of 40 pieces by him dating from 1792 or earlier are known today (though mainly from much later sources). Today his best-known works from before 1790 are three piano quartets and three piano sonatas, the quartets being closely modelled on Mozart's sonatas for piano and violin. From 1790 to 1802, his best music can be found in a cantata and a number of concert arias, and in some variations for solo piano, while his instrumental music (including movements of symphonies and a violin concerto, as well as various fragmentary chamber works) is conservative and uninspired.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The conventional "first period" begins after Beethoven's arrival in Vienna in 1792. In the first few years he seems to have composed less than he did at Bonn, and his Piano Trios, op.1 were not published until 1795. From this point onward, he had mastered the 'Viennese style' (best known today from <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Haydn" title="Joseph Haydn">Haydn</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wolfgang_Amadeus_Mozart" title="Wolfgang Amadeus Mozart">Mozart</a>) and was making the style his own. His works from 1795 to 1800 are larger in scale than was the norm (writing sonatas in four movements, not three, for instance); typically he uses a <a href="/wiki/Scherzo" title="Scherzo">scherzo</a> rather than a <a href="/wiki/Minuet_and_trio" class="mw-redirect" title="Minuet and trio">minuet and trio</a>; and his music often includes dramatic, even sometimes over-the-top, uses of extreme dynamics and tempi and chromatic harmony. It was this that led Haydn to believe the third trio of Op.1 was too difficult for an audience to appreciate.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroveOnlinesection_13_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroveOnlinesection_13-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>He also explored new directions and gradually expanded the scope and ambition of his work. Some important pieces from the early period are the <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._1_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 1 (Beethoven)">first</a> and <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._2_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 2 (Beethoven)">second</a> symphonies, the set of six string quartets <a href="/wiki/String_Quartets_Nos._1%E2%80%936,_Op._18_(Beethoven)" title="String Quartets Nos. 1–6, Op. 18 (Beethoven)">Opus 18</a>, the first two piano concertos, and the first dozen or so <a href="/wiki/Beethoven%27s_piano_sonatas" class="mw-redirect" title="Beethoven&#39;s piano sonatas">piano sonatas</a>, including the famous <i><a href="/wiki/Piano_Sonata_No._8_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Sonata No. 8 (Beethoven)">Pathétique</a></i> sonata, Op. 13.
</p><p>His middle (heroic) period began shortly after the personal crisis brought on by his recognition of encroaching deafness. It includes large-scale works that express heroism and struggle. Middle-period works include six symphonies (Nos. 3–8), the last two piano concertos, the <a href="/wiki/Triple_Concerto_(Beethoven)" title="Triple Concerto (Beethoven)">Triple Concerto</a> and <a href="/wiki/Violin_Concerto_(Beethoven)" title="Violin Concerto (Beethoven)">violin concerto</a>, five string quartets (Nos. 7–11), several piano sonatas (including the <i><a href="/wiki/Piano_Sonata_No._21_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Sonata No. 21 (Beethoven)">Waldstein</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Piano_Sonata_No._23_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Sonata No. 23 (Beethoven)">Appassionata</a></i> sonatas), the <i><a href="/wiki/Violin_Sonata_No._9_(Beethoven)" title="Violin Sonata No. 9 (Beethoven)">Kreutzer</a></i> violin sonata and his only <a href="/wiki/Opera" title="Opera">opera</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Fidelio" title="Fidelio">Fidelio</a></i>.
</p><p>Beethoven's late period began around 1815. Works from this period are characterised by their intellectual depth, their formal innovations, and their intense, highly personal expression. The <a href="/wiki/String_Quartet_No._14_(Beethoven)" title="String Quartet No. 14 (Beethoven)">String Quartet, Op. 131</a> has seven linked movements, and the <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._9_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 9 (Beethoven)">Ninth Symphony</a> adds choral forces to the orchestra in the last movement.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroveOnline_107-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroveOnline-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> Other compositions from this period include the <i><a href="/wiki/Missa_solemnis_(Beethoven)" title="Missa solemnis (Beethoven)">Missa solemnis</a></i>, the last five string quartets (including the massive <i><a href="/wiki/Gro%C3%9Fe_Fuge" title="Große Fuge">Große Fuge</a></i>) and the last five piano sonatas.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Character">Character</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Beethoven_walk.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Beethoven_walk.jpg/220px-Beethoven_walk.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="127" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Beethoven_walk.jpg/330px-Beethoven_walk.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Beethoven_walk.jpg/440px-Beethoven_walk.jpg 2x" data-file-width="787" data-file-height="454" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Beethoven_walk.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Beethoven's walk in nature, by <a href="/wiki/Julius_Schmid_(painter)" title="Julius Schmid (painter)">Julius Schmid</a></div></div></div>
<p>Beethoven's life was troubled by his encroaching loss of hearing and chronic abdominal pain since his twenties. He contemplated suicide as documented in his <a href="/wiki/Heiligenstadt_Testament" title="Heiligenstadt Testament">Heiligenstadt Testament</a>. He was often irascible. Nevertheless, he had a close and devoted circle of friends all his life, thought to have been attracted by his strength of personality. Towards the end of his life, his friends competed in their efforts to help him cope with his incapacities.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroveOnline_107-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroveOnline-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Sources show his disdain for authority and for social rank. He stopped performing at the piano if the audience chatted amongst themselves, or afforded him less than their full attention. At soirées, he refused to perform if suddenly called upon to do so. Eventually, after many confrontations, the Archduke Rudolph decreed that the usual rules of court etiquette did not apply to Beethoven.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroveOnline_107-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroveOnline-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>He was attracted to the ideals of the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Age of Enlightenment</a>. In 1804, when Napoleon's imperial ambitions became clear, Beethoven took hold of the title page of his <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._3_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 3 (Beethoven)">Third Symphony</a> and scratched the name Bonaparte out so violently that he made a hole in the paper. He later changed the work's title to "Sinfonia Eroica, composta per festeggiare il sovvenire d'un grand'uom" ("Heroic Symphony, composed to celebrate the memory of a great man"), and he rededicated it to his patron, Prince <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Franz_von_Lobkowitz" title="Joseph Franz von Lobkowitz">Joseph Franz von Lobkowitz</a>, at whose palace it was first performed.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The fourth movement of his <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._9_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 9 (Beethoven)">Ninth Symphony</a> features an elaborate choral setting of <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Schiller" title="Friedrich Schiller">Schiller's</a> Ode <i><a href="/wiki/Ode_to_Joy" title="Ode to Joy">An die Freude</a></i> ("Ode to Joy"), an optimistic hymn championing the brotherhood of humanity. In "Beethoven and Shakespeare: Ghosts and Heroes", David Roberts writes about the similarities between Beethoven's personality and Shakespeare's personality. The two men are known to be artistic colleagues, though from different ages in history, for their belief that their own understanding and rethinking of output which their own works entail can never come to a satisfactory end. Both were characterized by invention and reinvention. The expansion of their imaginative journeys had a propensity for joining comedy and tragedy, doing cogitation between rightness and strangeness, as they transcended their own selves in art, whereby nature had clear influences on them and on their works.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span></h2>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Beethoven_Monument_(Bonn)" title="Beethoven Monument (Bonn)">Beethoven Monument</a> in Bonn was unveiled in August 1845, in honour of his 75th anniversary. It was the first statue of a composer created in Germany, and the music festival that accompanied the unveiling was the impetus for the very hasty construction of the original <a href="/wiki/Beethovenhalle" title="Beethovenhalle">Beethovenhalle</a> in Bonn (it was designed and built within less than a month, on the urging of <a href="/wiki/Franz_Liszt" title="Franz Liszt">Franz Liszt</a>). A statue to Mozart had been unveiled in <a href="/wiki/Salzburg" title="Salzburg">Salzburg</a>, Austria in 1842. Vienna did not honour Beethoven with a statue until 1880.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> His is the only name inscribed on one of the plaques that trim <a href="/wiki/Symphony_Hall,_Boston" title="Symphony Hall, Boston">Symphony Hall, Boston</a>; the others were left empty because it was felt that only Beethoven's popularity would endure.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There is a museum, the <a href="/wiki/Beethoven_House" title="Beethoven House">Beethoven House</a>, the place of his birth, in central Bonn. The same city has hosted a musical festival, the <a href="/wiki/Beethovenfest" title="Beethovenfest">Beethovenfest</a>, since 1845. The festival was initially irregular but has been organised annually since 2007.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Ira_F._Brilliant_Center_for_Beethoven_Studies" title="Ira F. Brilliant Center for Beethoven Studies">Ira F. Brilliant Center for Beethoven Studies</a> serves as a museum, research center, and host of lectures and performances devoted solely to this life and works.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Beethoven_(crater)" title="Beethoven (crater)">third largest crater</a> on Mercury is named in his honour, as is the main-belt asteroid <a href="/wiki/1815_Beethoven" title="1815 Beethoven">1815 Beethoven</a>.
</p>

<li id="cite_note-landrecht-90"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-landrecht_90-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-landrecht_90-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">On 18 December 1818, the Landrechte, the Austrian court for the nobility, handed over the whole matter of guardianship to the Vienna Magistrate, the court for commoners. "It&#160;... appears from the statement of Ludwig van Beethoven, as the accompanying copy of the court minutes of 11&#160;December of this year shows, that he is unable to prove nobility: hence the matter of guardianship is transferred to an honorable magistrate."</span>

Less impressive, in these years, is the instrumental music in the sonata style. ... Where Beethoven departed from formula in these works he seems to have straggled helplessly, as in the violin concerto fragment. ... Beethoven at Bonn was a less interesting composer of works in the sonata style than of music in other genres ..."</span>
